REGULAR POISSON MANIFOLDS OF COMPACT TYPES

(PMCT 2)

MARIUS CRAINIC, RUI LOJA FERNANDES, AND DAVID MARTÂ´INEZ TORRES

Abstract. This is the second paper of a series dedicated to the study of
Poisson structures of compact types (PMCTs).
In this paper, we focus on
regular PMCTs, exhibiting a rich transverse geometry. We show that their
leaf spaces are integral aï¬ƒne orbifolds. We prove that the cohomology class of
the leafwise symplectic form varies linearly and that there is a distinguished
polynomial function describing the leafwise sympletic volume. The leaf space
of a PMCT carries a natural Duistermaat-Heckman measure and a Weyl type
integration formula holds. We introduce the notion of a symplectic gerbe,
and we show that they obstruct realizing PMCTs as the base of a symplectic
complete isotropic ï¬bration (a.k.a. a non-commutative integrable system).

1. Introduction

This is the second paper of a series of works devoted to the study of Poisson
structures of compact types (PMCTs). These are the analogues in Poisson Geometry
of compact Lie groups in Lie theory. In the ï¬rst paper of this series [20] we have
discussed general properties, described several examples, and outlined our general
plan. In this paper, which is self-contained, we focus on regular PMCTs and we
discover a very rich transverse geometry, where several structures, both classical
and new, interact with each other in a non-trivial way. These include orbifold

MC and DMT were partially supported by the NWO Vici Grant no. 639.033.312. RLF was
partially supported by NSF grants DMS 13-08472 and DMS 14-05671, FCT/Portugal and the
CiË†encias Sem Fronteiras program.

structures, integral aï¬ƒne structures, symplectic gerbes, etc. Moreover, we ï¬nd that
celebrated results, like the Duistermaat-Heckman Theorem on the linear variation of
the symplectic class in the cohomology of reduced spaces, the polynomial behavior
of the Duistermaat-Heckman measure, the Atiyah-Guillemin-Sternberg Convexity
Theorem, or the Weyl Integration Formula, ï¬t perfectly into the world of PMCTs,
arising as particular statements of general results concerning PMCTs.

Given a Poisson manifold (M, Ï€) we will look at s-connected integrations (G, â„¦),
which are symplectic Lie groupoids of compact type. At the level of Lie groupoids,
there are several compact types C characterized by possible conditions on G:

C âˆˆ {proper, s-proper, compact},

(1.1)

that is, Hausdorï¬€ Lie groupoids with proper anchor map, proper source map, and
compact manifold of arrows, respectively. For example, when G = G Ã— M comes
from a Lie group acting on a manifold M , the three conditions correspond to the
properness of the action, the compactness of G, and the compactness of both G
and M , respectively. Therefore, one says that the Poisson manifold (M, Ï€) is of:

â€¢ C-type if it has an s-connected integration (G, â„¦) with property C;
â€¢ strong C-type if its canonical integration Î£(M, Ï€) has property C.

A Poisson manifold (M, Ï€) comes with a partition into symplectic leaves, gener-
alizing the partition by coadjoint orbits from Lie theory. In this paper, we consider
PMCTs where the dimension of the leaves is constant, leaving the non-regular case
to the next paper in the series [21]. This gives rise to a regular foliation FÏ€ on M ,
so, in some sense, we are looking at symplectic foliations from the perspective of
Poisson Geometry.

For a general regular Poisson manifold, the leaf space

B = M/FÏ€

is very pathological. However, for us, the ï¬rst immediate consequence of any of
the compactness conditions is that B is Hausdorï¬€. Moreover, we will see that it
comes with a very rich geometry, illustrated in the following theorem, which collects
several results spread throughout the paper:

Theorem 1.0.1. Given a regular Poisson manifold (M, Ï€) of proper type and an
s-connected, proper symplectic integration (G, â„¦):
(a) The space B of symplectic leaves comes with an orbifold structure B = B(G);
(b) There is an induced integral aï¬ƒne structure Î› on B;
(c) The classical eï¬€ective orbifold underlying B is good;
(d) There is a symplectic T -gerbe over B, where T is the symplectic torus bundle
induced by Î›. This gerbe is classiï¬ed by the Lagrangian Dixmier-Douady class:

c2(G, â„¦) âˆˆ H 2(B, TLagr).

(e) The class c2(G, â„¦) vanishes if and only if (M, Ï€) admits a proper isotropic
realization q : (X, â„¦X) â†’ (M, Ï€) for which G âˆ¼= BX (M, Ï€), a natural symplectic
integration constructed from X and the orbifold structure B.

The presence of an orbifold structure on the leaf space which, in general, is non-
eï¬€ective, gives rise to several technical diï¬ƒculties throughout the discussion. When
the symplectic leaves are 1-connected, then B is just a smooth manifold, and no
further complications arise from orbifolds. In this case, all the other main features
of PMCTs are already present, and it includes interesting examples, such as the

PMCT 2

3

regular coadjoint orbits or the principal conjugacy classes of a compact Lie group.
For that reason, in the general discussion we will often consider this case ï¬rst.

The diï¬€erent geometric structures present on the leaf space of a PMCT, men-
tioned in the previous theorem, interact nicely with the leafwise symplectic geome-
try. One illustration of this interaction is the linear variation of symplectic forms
in cohomology, generalizing the classical Duistermaat-Heckman Theorem. For sim-
plicity, we concentrate on the smooth case, where the leaves are 1-connected. Then
to each b âˆˆ B corresponds a symplectic leaf (Sb, Ï‰b), and the cohomologies H 2(Sb)

One can also look at volume forms instead. Assume as before that we have

The rest of this paper is organized into 8 sections and 2 appendices.
Section 2 is devoted to foliations and orbifolds, recalling Haeï¬‚igerâ€™s approach
to transversal geometry, ï¬xing the necessary framework, but also illustrating the
various compactness properties (1.1) in the simpler context of foliations. In this
section, the orbifold structure on the leaf space of a PMCT, stated in part (a) of
Theorem 1.0.1, is shown to exist.

Section 3 includes some basics on Integral Aï¬ƒne Geometry and describes its
relationship with Poisson Geometry. Besides proving part (b) of Theorem 1.0.1, we
discover new Poisson invariants, the so-called extended monodromy groups which
give rise to obstructions to s-properness, but which are interesting also for general
Poisson manifolds.

Sections 4 and Section 5 concern Theorem 1.0.2, on the linear variation of the
cohomology class of the leafwise symplectic form. We ï¬rst treat the case of smooth
leaf space and then the orbifold case. Both these sections start by revisiting the
developing map for integral aï¬ƒne structures from a novel groupoid perspective.
That allows for a global formulation, free of choices, which is more appropriate
for our purposes. We also obtain a decomposition result for Poisson manifolds of
s-proper type which, from the point of view of classiï¬cation, indicates two types of
building blocks: (i) the strong proper ones with full variation, and (ii) the ones with
no variation, corresponding to symplectic ï¬brations over integral aï¬ƒne manifolds.
Section 6 discusses the Duistermaat-Heckman measures on PMCTs and on their
leaf spaces, its relationship with the measures determined by the integral aï¬ƒne
structures, and the interaction with the Liouville measure on the symplectic leaves,
leading to proofs of Theorem 1.0.3, on the polynomial nature of the Duistermaat-
Heckman measure, and the integration formula of Theorem 1.0.4.

Section 7 explains the relationship between PMCTs and proper isotropic real-
izations, which appears in part (e) of Theorem 1.0.1. For any proper isotropic
realization q : (X, â„¦X) â†’ (M, Ï€) we introduce a â€œholonomy groupoid relative to
Xâ€, HolX (M, Ï€), which is usually smaller than the canonical integration Î£(M, Ï€),
and hence has better chances to be proper. The groupoids HolX (M, Ï€) not only
arise in many examples, but are an important concept. Indeed, recall that folia-
tions come with two standard s-connected integrations: the largest one which is
the monodromy groupoid Mon(M, F ) and the smallest one which is the holonomy
groupoid Hol(M, F ). In Poisson geometry, the integration Î£(M, Ï€) is the analogue
of Mon(M, F ) but, in general, there is no analogue of the holonomy groupoid. Our

PMCT 2

5

results suggest that, in Poisson Geometry, instead of looking for the smallest in-
tegration, one should look for the smallest one that acts on a given symplectic
realization. This property characterizes HolX (M, Ï€) uniquely.

Sections 8 and 9 describe our theory of symplectic gerbes, ï¬rst in the smooth
case and then in the orbifold case, proving parts (d) and (e) of Theorem 1.0.1.
Our departure point, is the usual theory of S1-gerbes, which we ï¬rst extend to T -
gerbes, where T is an arbitrary torus bundle over a manifold B. For the symplectic
theory, we need to look at a symplectic torus bundles (T , Ï‰T ) over an orbifold B or,
equivalently, integral aï¬ƒne structures on B. In the more standard theory ones looks
at central extensions of T by Lie groupoids, while in the symplectic theory we look
at central extensions of (T , Ï‰T ) by symplectic groupoids. The main conclusion
is that, while T -gerbes are classiï¬ed by their Dixmier-Douady classes, living in
H 2(B, T ), for symplectic gerbes one obtains a Lagrangian Dixmier-Douady class
which gives rise to a group isomorphism

c2 : GerbB(T , Ï‰T ) â†’ H 2(B, T Lagr),

where T Lagr is the sheaf of Lagrangian sections of (T , Ï‰T ).

Appendix A gives some background on actions of symplectic groupoids, Hamil-
tonian G-spaces, and symplectic Morita equivalence, which are relevant for the
paper. Appendix B is of a very diï¬€erent nature: we show there how one can adapt
(part of) Molinoâ€™s approach of Riemannian foliations to the context of integral
aï¬ƒne geometry, to prove that integral aï¬ƒne orbifolds are good, i.e. quotients of
a discrete integral aï¬ƒne group action. While this is relevant for PMCTs and we
make good use of it, we believe it may be of independent interest.

As we develop the theory of PMCTs, we will explain how to adapt it to Dirac
manifolds. The ï¬rst motivation for this arises from the extension of the results
of this paper from regular to arbitrary PMCTs, since we will introduce in [21] a
desingularization procedure which will turn a PMCT into a regular, Dirac manifold
(without changing the leaf space or the compactness type!). The second motiva-
tion comes from Lie theory and the striking similarity between the geometry of
(co)adjoint orbits and the one of conjugacy classes (see e.g. [30]). While coadjoint
orbits ï¬t into Poisson Geometry, conjugacy classes belong to the world of (twisted)
Dirac Geometry. Hence the Dirac framework allows us to (re)cover even more
fundamental examples.

However, in order to get a faster grasp of the results and new techniques intro-
duced here, the reader may choose to skip, in a ï¬rst reading, all sections concerning
Dirac Geometry. The same applies to the sections on orbifolds, since the rich geo-
metry that comes with PMCTs is present already when the leaf space is smooth.

Acknowledgments. The work of N.-T. Zung [66] on proper symplectic grou-
poids should be considered as a precursor of the theory of PMCTs. However, Zung
focus his attention on the symplectic groupoid, instead of the underlying Poisson
manifold. A. Weinsteinâ€™s work on measures on stacks [64] was a source of inspiration
for our study of measures. Our theory of symplectic gerbes can be viewed as a

a richer geometric ï¬‚avor that includes the connection to the Delzant-Dazord theory
of isotropic ï¬brations [26]. We would also like to acknowledge various discussions

2. PMCTs, foliations of compact types and orbifolds

Recall that given a foliation F on M the associated distribution can be thought
of as a Lie algebroid with anchor the inclusion and bracket the restriction of the
Lie bracket of vector ï¬elds. This Lie algebroid is well-known to be integrable
â€“ for example by the holonomy groupoid (see Section 2.1). Therefore, for any
of the compactness types (1.1), the notion of C-type (respectively, strong C-type)
makes sense for any foliated manifold (M, F ): one requires the existence of a source
connected (respectively, source 1-connected) Hausdorï¬€ Lie groupoid integrating F
having property C.

In this section we shall make a detailed study of these compactness types of folia-
tions. On the one hand, foliations of compact types are easier to handle than regular
Poisson manifolds of compact types, but they still exhibit phenomena/properties
that will persist in the Poisson case. Thus the analysis of the former will play a
guiding role in the analysis of the latter. On the other hand, if a regular Pois-
son manifolds is of (strong) C-type, then so is the underlying symplectic foliation.
Hence, the results in this section have immediate applications to the Poisson case.

As (rough) main goal of this section, we mention here:

Theorem 2.0.5. If (M, Ï€) is a regular Poisson manifold of C-type, then the sym-
plectic foliation FÏ€ is of C-type. As a consequence, the space of symplectic leaves

B = M/FÏ€

is an orbifold. More precisely, any integration G of (M, Ï€) of C-type gives rise to
an integration B(G) of FÏ€ of C-type, which induces an orbifold structure on B.

We shall see in the next sections that symplectic integrations of (M, Ï€) induce
several geometric structures on the orbifold B. For that reason this section pays
special attention to geometric structures on leaf spaces of foliations and on orbifolds.

Remark 2.0.6 (Classical compact foliations). In classical Foliation Theory the
notion of a compact foliation refers to a foliation all whose leaves are compact (see,
e.g., [31, 32]). This property does not refer to any of the integrations of F . We
will clarify later how this classical notion is related to our compactness types. In
this regard, since the pioneering work of A. Haeï¬‚iger, Lie groupoids (in particular,
the holonomy groupoid) have been extensively used in the study of the transverse
geometry of foliations. Our approach, follows the same spirit, deï¬ning compactness
type of foliations in terms of groupoids integrating them.

2.1. The monodromy and holonomy groupoids. The Lie groupoids that in-
tegrate foliations are called foliation groupoids. They are easy to characterize,
since, in general, the Lie algebra of the isotropy group of a Lie groupoid is precisely
the kernel of the anchor of its Lie algebroid:

Proposition 2.1.1 ([24]). A Lie groupoid G is a foliation groupoid iï¬€ all the iso-
tropy groups Gx are discrete.

Since any foliation F on M is integrable as a Lie algebroid, it has a unique
(smooth) source 1-connected integration, called the the monodromy groupoid
of F and denoted by

Mon(M, F ) â‡’ M.

The arrows in this groupoid are the leafwise homotopy classes (relative to the end-
points) of leafwise curves in M (see [52]).

PMCT 2

7

Every foliation has yet another s-connected canonical integration: the holo-

nomy groupoid of F , denoted by

Hol(M, F ) â‡’ M.

The arrows are now equivalence classes of leafwise paths where two paths are iden-
tiï¬ed if they induce the same germ of holonomy transformation. From their deï¬ni-
tions, we have a morphism of Lie groupoids which is a local diï¬€eomorphism:

hol : Mon(M, F ) â†’ Hol(M, F ).

(2.1)

The relevance of the holonomy groupoid in studying the transverse geometry of
foliations stems from the fact that any other s-connected Lie groupoid integrating
F lies above it. More precisely:

Theorem 2.1.2 ([24, 56]). For any s-connected integration E of a foliation F on M ,
there is a natural factorization of (2.1) into a composition of surjective submersions
compatible with the groupoid structure:

Mon(M, F )

holE /

/ Hol(M, F ) .

Recall that we are only interested in Hausdorï¬€ Lie groupoids, although even
very elementary foliations can have non-Hausdorï¬€ monodromy and/or holonomy
groupoids. Moreover, one can have one of them being Hausdorï¬€ while the other
one is not, and even both not being Hausdorï¬€ but there exists a Hausdorï¬€ one
in between them! The monodromy groupoid is Hausdorï¬€ iï¬€ the foliation does not
have vanishing cycles [2], but no geometric criteria characterizing the Hausdorï¬€ness
of other foliation groupoids (e.g., the holonomy groupoid) is known.

Example 2.1.3 (Simple foliations). If p : M â†’ B is a submersion with connected
ï¬bers, then the ï¬bers of p deï¬ne a foliation F on M , called a simple foliation. The
holonomy groupoid of F is the submersion groupoid of p, consisting of pairs of
points in M that are in the same ï¬ber of p:

Hol(M, F ) = M Ã—B M â‡’ M,

where (x, y) âˆˆ M Ã—B M is thought of as an arrow from y to x. This groupoid is
Hausdorï¬€ but the monodromy groupoid of F may fail to be Hausdorï¬€: e.g., the
ï¬ber above 0 of the ï¬rst projection p : R3 \ {0} â†’ R contains a vanishing cycle.

2.2. Foliation versus Â´etale groupoids. Recall that an Â´etale groupoid is a Lie
groupoid whose source map is a local diï¬€eomorphism. Typical examples of Â´etale
groupoids include:

â€¢ the identity groupoid M â‡’ M of a manifold,
â€¢ the action groupoid associated to a discrete group action on a manifold.

The fundamental example coming from foliation theory is the restriction of the
holonomy groupoid of (M, F ) to a complete transversal T (i.e., a transversal inter-
secting all the leaves):

Hol(M, F )|T

â‡’ T.

Historically, Â´etale groupoids associated with a foliation were introduced via pseu-
dogroups, as the objects that encode the transverse geometry of the foliation (see
Remark 2.3.3 below). The main point about Â´etale groupoids is that they can be
handled very much as usual manifolds. The resulting theory should be viewed as
a study of â€œsingular spacesâ€, namely, the orbit spaces of the Â´etale groupoids. The
role of the Â´etale groupoid is to provide a â€œdesingularizationâ€ of the singular space.

A foliation groupoid E â‡’ M and the Â´etale groupoid

â‡’ T )

2.4. Foliations of C-types. We turn now to the study of foliations of compact
types. Any such foliation has linear holonomy. In fact, we have:

Lemma 2.4.1. Let (M, F ) be a foliation of proper (respectively, s-proper) type.
Then its leaves are closed embedded (respectively, compact) submanifolds, the holo-
nomy groups are ï¬nite, and the orbit space is Hausdorï¬€. If (M, F ) is strong proper,
then the leaves have ï¬nite fundamental group.

Proof. A simple topological argument implies that for a proper (respectively, s-
proper) Lie groupoid all the orbits are closed embedded (respectively, compact)
submanifolds, the isotropy groups are compact, and the orbit space, furnished with
the quotient topology, is Hausdorï¬€ (see e.g.
[25, 63, 66]). For an s-connected
integration E of F , the isotropy groups of E surject onto the holonomy groups. (cid:3)

12

MARIUS CRAINIC, RUI LOJA FERNANDES, AND DAVID MARTÂ´INEZ TORRES

While strong C-types were deï¬ned using the monodromy groupoids, we claim

that the C-types can be checked using the holonomy groupoids:

Theorem 2.4.2. A foliation is of C-type iï¬€ its holonomy groupoid has property C.

The proof of Theorem 2.4.2 is deferred until the next section, where we discuss
normal forms for foliations. For now, we look at some examples. One should keep in
mind that a foliation (M, F ) is of compact type iï¬€ it is s-proper and M is compact.

Example 2.4.3. A simple foliation (M, F ) as in Example 2.1.3 is always of proper
type, it is of s-proper type iï¬€ p is proper and it is of compact type iï¬€ M is compact.
Example 2.4.4. For the linear foliation ( Ë†S Ã—Î“ V, Flin) the explicit integrations
(2.3) and (2.4) together with Proposition 2.1.6 imply that Flin is:

â€¢ proper (respectively, s-proper) iï¬€ Î“ is ï¬nite (respectively, Î“ is ï¬nite and S

is compact);

â€¢ strong proper (respectively, strong s-proper) iï¬€ Ï€1(S) is ï¬nite (respectively,

Ï€1(S) is ï¬nite and S is compact);

â€¢ never of compact type.

Note that in the deï¬nition of C-type one requires the foliation groupoid to be

s-connected. One should be aware of the following phenomena:

(i) If a foliation groupoid E â‡’ M is proper, then passing to its source connected

component E 0 âŠ‚ E may destroy properness.

(ii) If a foliation (M, F ) is of proper type and U âŠ‚ M is open, then (U, F|U ) may

fail to be of proper type.

In fact, notice that for a foliation (M, F ) and an open set U âŠ‚ M , the leaves
of F|U are the connected components of the intersections of the leaves of F with
U . Although the restriction Hol(M, F )|U still integrates F|U , it may fail to be
s-connected. Passing to the associated s-connected groupoid, one gets precisely the
holonomy groupoid of F|U :

Hol(U, F|U ) = (Hol(M, F )|U )0.

(2.7)

Examples illustrating (i) and (ii) can then be constructed even starting from a
simple foliation. For instance, consider M = R2 with the foliation induced by the
second projection and restrict it to U = R2 \ {0}. Then (U, F|U ) is not proper
because its leaf space is not Hausdorï¬€. In particular, Hol(M, F )|U is proper while
its source connected component is not.

Lemma 2.4.1 gives necessary conditions for the properness of a foliation, but

they are not suï¬ƒcient as illustrated by the next two examples:

Example 2.4.5. There are foliations where all leaves are embedded, the holonomy
groups are ï¬nite, and the leaf space is Hausdorï¬€, but are not of proper type:
consider the linear foliation of the MÂ¨obius band M by circles. The middle circle C
is the only leaf with non-trivial holonomy.

Restrict now this foliation to the open U obtained from M by removing one
point in C. In this way, the leaf space remains unchanged, is Hausdorï¬€, and the
leaves clearly have the desired properties. However, the holonomy groupoid is not
proper, as can be seen by considering the holonomy group of the initial foliation,
given by (2.3), and then restricting as in (2.7). From Theorem 2.4.2 we conclude
that (U, F|U ) is not of proper type.

Example 2.4.6. There are foliations where all leaves are embedded, the homotopy

2.5. Normal forms. We start with the the standard local Reeb stability:

(U, F|U )

Proof. We are left to prove the converse implication. For that notice that it suï¬ƒces

(cid:3)

(a) All leaves have ï¬nite holonomy.

2.6. Orbifolds. Proper foliation groupoids serve as atlases for orbifold structures:

Deï¬nition 2.6.1. Let B be a topological space. An orbifold atlas on B is a pair
(B, p) consisting of:

â€¢ a proper foliation groupoid B â‡’ M ;
â€¢ a homeomorphism p : M/Bâ†’B between the space of orbits of B and B.

An orbifold is a pair (B, B) consisting of a space B and an orbifold atlas B on B.

Two orbifold atlases (Bi, pi), i âˆˆ {1, 2}, are said to be equivalent if there exists
a Morita equivalence B1 â‰ƒ B2 with the property that the induced homeomorphism
on the orbit spaces is compatible with p1 and p2.

It follows from the previous discussion that, by passing to a transversal T , one
can always use orbifold atlases which are Â´etale. While nowadays one uses general
foliation groupoids [50], the ï¬rst approaches to orbifolds via groupoids used only
Â´etale atlases (see e.g. [1]). Although Â´etale atlases are often advantageous, restricting
to them is unnatural, not only conceptually, but also from the point of view of
concrete examples. For example, quotients M/G of proper, locally free, actions of
Lie groups come with an obvious choice of orbifold atlas (the action groupoid), but
not with a canonical Â´etale one. PMCTs will provide similar examples.

Remark 2.6.2. In the existing literature, orbifolds are often deï¬ned as â€œa space
with an equivalence class of orbifold atlasesâ€, while an atlas is interpreted as a
â€œpresentationâ€ of the orbifold [50, 1]. However, note that:

â€¢ two equivalent atlases can be equivalent in many, very distinct, ways, and
â€¢ two diï¬€erent equivalences give rise to diï¬€erent ways of passing from one

atlas to the other.

Hence, not ï¬xing an atlas gives rise to subtleties. This shows up already when
deï¬ning, in an atlas-independent way, a morphism between orbifolds, or a vector
bundle over an orbifold. This kind of problems can be solved by developing a
rather heavy categorical language [44]. In practice, we do not have to deal with
such issues and all orbifolds arise with a canonical orbifold atlas, in the sense of
our deï¬nition. Given an orbifold (B, B), it is sometimes advantageous to pass to a
more convenient atlas E: however, such a passing will always be done via a speciï¬ed
Morita equivalence QE : E â‰ƒ B.

Example 2.6.3 (Manifolds and smooth orbifolds). Any manifold B can be seen
as an orbifold by using the trivial groupoid B â‡’ B as an atlas. Such an orbifold
is called a smooth orbifold. Note that an orbifold is smooth precisely when
a/any deï¬ning atlas has no isotropy. Other equivalent Â´etale atlases are provided by
choosing a manifold atlas {Ui, Ï†i}iâˆˆI for B and considering the associated covering

groupoid, i.e. the Â´etale groupoid with space of objects the disjoint union`i Ui and

one arrow from (x, i) to (x, j) for each x âˆˆ Ui âˆ© Uj.

On the other hand, for any orbifold B one may also talk about the smoothness
of the underlying topological space: one requires the topological space B to
admit a smooth structure such that, for some orbifold atlas B â‡’ M , the quotient
map p : M â†’ B is a submersion. This condition does not depend on the choice of
the atlas and determines a unique smooth structure on B, if it exists.

Obviously, for a smooth orbifold the underlying topological space is smooth.
However, it is important to keep in mind that the underlying topological space
maybe smooth while the orbifold itself may still fail to be a smooth orbifold, for

PMCT 2

17

the orbifold atlases may have non-trivial isotropy. A simple example is obtained by
taking the action groupoid Î“ â‹‰ B â‡’ B associated with a trivial action of a ï¬nite
group. Our study of PMCTs will give rise to much more interesting examples-
where the isotropy information is important and cannot be disregarded.

Example 2.6.4 (Classical orbifolds). Originally, orbifold structures on a space B
were deï¬ned in complete analogy with smooth structures, but using charts that
identify the opens in B with quotients Rn/Î“ of ï¬nite groups Î“ acting eï¬€ectively on
Rn [58, 61]. With the appropriate compatibility between such charts, one obtains
the notion of a classical orbifold atlas. Similarly to the covering groupoids above
for manifolds, such an atlas can be organized into a proper Â´etale groupoid whose
space of orbits is B [1, 52]. Therefore, the classical notion of orbifold can be seen as
particular classes of orbifolds in the sense of Deï¬nition 2.6.1. Here, following [1, 50],
we will adopt the following equivalent working deï¬nition: a classical orbifold is
an orbifold for which the deï¬ning atlas is eï¬€ective.

Notice that the subtleties related to orbifolds atlases mentioned in Remark 2.6.2

are not present in the case of classical orbifolds:

Lemma 2.6.5. For a classical orbifold B, an equivalence Q : B1 â‰ƒ B2 between two
orbifold atlases for B is unique up to isomorphism.

Since any (proper) Â´etale groupoid has an associated eï¬€ective (proper) Â´etale
groupoid, we see that any orbifold has an underlying classical orbifold struc-
ture. In this terminology, the smoothness of the underlying topological space of
an orbifold is equivalent to the condition that its underlying classical orbifold is
smooth. A general orbifold structure can be seen as a classical orbifold together
with extra data, which is codiï¬ed in the isotropy groups of the orbifold atlases.

Example 2.6.6 (Good orbifolds). A large class of examples of orbifolds arise as
quotients M/Î“ for proper actions of discrete groups Î“: the action groupoid Î“ â‹‰ M
gives an orbifold atlas. Orbifolds of this type are called good orbifolds [1, 61].

Example 2.6.7 (Foliations of C-type and orbifolds). For a foliation (M, F ) of C-
type, any s-connected, proper integration E â‡’ M makes the leaf space B = M/F
into an orbifold (B, E). Diï¬€erent integrations give diï¬€erent orbifold structures.
However, the holonomy groupoid Hol(M, F ) â‡’ M provides a smallest integration
(Theorem 2.1.2), which is proper (Theorem 2.4.2) and eï¬€ective (Proposition 2.2.1).
Hence, the underlying classical orbifold of any orbifold deï¬ned by a foliation (M, F )
of C-type has atlas Hol(M, F ) â‡’ M .

Remark 2.6.8 (Geometric structures on orbifolds). Haeï¬‚igerâ€™s approach to trans-
verse structures, discussed in Remark 2.3.3, when applied to the orbifold atlases
allows one to consider various geometric structures on orbifolds, such as vector
bundles, diï¬€erential forms, Riemannian structures, etc: if S is a Haeï¬‚iger sheaf on
Rq then S(B) for a q-dimensional orbifold (B, B) is deï¬ned by applying S to the
orbifold atlas B â‡’ M . Given some other atlas QE : E â‰ƒ B we have an induced
isomorphism S(E) âˆ¼= S(B), which in general depends on the Morita equivalence QE.

We can now return to regular PMCTs, the conclusion being that their leaf spaces
are orbifolds. More precisely, each s-connected, proper integration gives rise to a
orbifold structure on the leaf space, so one has the following more precise version
of Theorem 2.0.5:

3. Integral affine structure

Integral aï¬ƒne structures form another type of geometric structure that plays a
crucial role in the study of compactness in Poisson Geometry. We initiate their
study in this section. First, we start by recalling some basic deï¬nitions and proper-
ties of integral aï¬ƒne structures on manifolds. Then we discuss transverse integral
aï¬ƒne structures on foliated manifolds and their relation to integral aï¬ƒne struc-
tures on orbifolds. This will set the stage to prove the main result of this section,
which improves on the orbifold structure on the leaf spaces of PMCTs constructed
in Theorem 2.6.9. A simpliï¬ed version can be stated as follows:

Theorem 3.0.10. For any regular Poisson manifold (M, Ï€) of C-type its leaf space
B = M/FÏ€ is an integral aï¬ƒne orbifold: any s-connected symplectic integration
(G, â„¦) of (M, Ï€) having property C gives rise to an integral orbifold structure on B.
Moreover, the underlying classical orbifold is good.

For a foliated manifold (M, F ), a transverse integral aï¬ƒne structure is described
by a collection of subgroups of its conormal bundle Î½âˆ—(F ), as will be recalled below.
On the other hand, the monodromy groups of a Poisson manifold [18], an invariant
which characterizes its integrability, is another collection of subgroups of Î½âˆ—(FÏ€).
Another goal of this section is to describe the role of the monodromy groups in
the integral aï¬ƒne geometry of PMCTs. In this study, a new invariant of Poisson
structures, called the extended monodromy groups, will emerge.

Integral aï¬ƒne structures look, at ï¬rst, deceivingly simple. However, even some
of the simplest questions are surprisingly hard to answer. For instance, we mention
here an integral aï¬ƒne version of an old conjecture in aï¬ƒne geometry:

Conjecture 3.1.4 (Markus conjecture â€“ integral aï¬ƒne version). Any compact in-
tegral aï¬ƒne manifold must be complete, i.e. of the form Rq/Î“ for some smooth
integral aï¬ƒne subgroup Î“ âŠ‚ Aï¬€ Z(Rq).

Remark 3.1.5 (Aï¬ƒne structures). Aï¬ƒne structures on B are deï¬ned, via atlases,
as above, except that the changes of coordinates (3.1) are only aï¬ƒne (i.e., v âˆˆ Rq
and A âˆˆ GL(Rq)). The analogue of the 1-1 correspondence from the previous
proposition, states that they correspond to ï¬‚at torsion free connections on T B. In
this context one can talk about invariant (or parallel) densities and volume forms
by requiring invariance with respect to parallel transport of the connection. The
standard Markus conjecture states that a compact aï¬ƒne structure with an invariant
density has a complete connection.

Integral aï¬ƒne structures always admit invariant positive densities (or volume
forms, in the orientable case): one sets Âµ := |dx1 âˆ§ . . . âˆ§ dxq| for any choice of
integral aï¬ƒne local coordinates (x1, . . . , xn). An interesting question that seems to
be still open is whether, conversely, any aï¬ƒne structure that admits an invariant
density comes from an integral aï¬ƒne structure.

A integral structure EZ on a vector bundle E â†’ B gives rise to a bundle of tori
T := E/EZ. Conversely, given a bundle of tori T â†’ B, the Lie algebras of the
ï¬bers give rise to a vector bundle E â†’ B, while the kernels of the exponential maps
give rise to an integral structure EZ on E such that T â‰¡ E/EZ.

3.2. Integral aï¬ƒne structures on orbifolds and foliations. We deï¬ne integral
aï¬ƒne structures on orbifolds following Haeï¬‚igerâ€™s approach (see Remark 2.6.8):

Deï¬nition 3.2.1. An integral aï¬ƒne structure on a orbifold (B, B) is an
integral aï¬ƒne structure on the base of some Â´etale orbifold atlas E â‡’ T which is
invariant under the action (2.6) by elements of E .

This deï¬nition only uses the linear part of the action (2.6), so an integral aï¬ƒne
structure on an orbifold is the same things as one on its underlying classical orbifold.
For this reason, the Morita equivalence QE : E â‰ƒ B plays here no role (see Lemma
2.6.5): any Morita equivalence between two atlases allows us to move an invariant
integral aï¬ƒne structure from one base to the other (pull-back to the bibundle, then
push forward by the obvious quotient operation), and the result does not depend
on the choice of equivalence.

To represent the integral aï¬ƒne structure in arbitrary, possibly non-Â´etale, orbifold
atlases, we need the notion of transverse integral aï¬ƒne structure. Recall that for

the plaques of F in Ui.

of codimension q is a choice of a maximal foliation atlas {(Ui, Ï†i) : i âˆˆ I} with the
property that each transition function

is (the restriction of) an integral aï¬ƒne transformation in Aï¬€ Z(Rq) = GLq(Z) â‹‰ Rq.
A chart (Ui, Ï†i) is called a transverse integral aï¬ƒne coordinate system.

More eï¬ƒcient descriptions of transverse integral aï¬ƒne structure can be given in

Example 3.2.5 (Simple foliations). If (M, F ) is simple, then transverse integral
aï¬ƒne structures on F are in 1-1 correspondence with integral aï¬ƒne structures on
the smooth manifold B = M/F . In terms of lattices, they are related via pullback
by p : M â†’ B.

Example 3.2.6 (Orbifolds). For foliations (M, F ) of proper type we know that the
leaf space B = M/F is an orbifold (see Example 2.6.7). We now have a bijection

Strictly speaking, one has several orbifold structures on B, one for each proper
s-connected integration E of F . However, as we remarked before, the notion of
integral aï¬ƒne structure only depends on the underlying classical orbifold.

Starting with an arbitrary orbifold B, the previous example is relevant to the
way one can represent integral aï¬ƒne structures on B with respect to arbitrary
orbifold atlases E â‡’ M (not necessarily Â´etale ones). While in this case E may
have disconnected s-ï¬bers, we have to consider transverse integral aï¬ƒne structures

condition is superï¬‚uous if E is s-connected). Such Î›s will be called E-invariant
(transverse) integral aï¬ƒne.

3.5. Examples. Here are some examples to illustrate the behavior of the groups
Nmon and Nhol, their computation and their relevance to the compactness-types.

3.5.1. A non-proper example with Nhol discrete. The groups Nhol can be seen as
Poisson invariants whose discreteness is a necessary condition for properness- cf.
Corollary 3.4.11. Here is an example which shows that this condition is not suï¬ƒcient
and that Nhol provides interesting invariants also in the non-proper case. Start with
the Reeb foliation F of S3 and make it into a symplectic foliation by choosing a
metric on S3 and considering the induced area forms on the leaves. The resulting
Poisson structure is not of proper type since the Reeb foliation is not proper: for
example, the linear and non-linear holonomy of the compact leaf are distinct.

These Reeb type Poisson structures are always integrable since for any symplectic
leaf S we have Ï€2(S, x) = {0}, so that Nmon = {0}. On the other hand, for points x
in the open leaves we obviously have Nhol|x = {0}, since the leaves are contractible.
However, we claim that for points in the compact leaf, Nhol is not trivial. In order
to see this, we consider as a model for a neighborhood of the compact leaf T2 the
space M = (R Ã— S1 Ã— R)/Z, and we let R+ Ã— S1 Ã— R, with coordinates (r, Î¸, z), be

3.5.3. The free Hamiltonian T -spaces perspective. A fundamental tool to construct
Poisson structures is by Hamiltonian reduction of symplectic manifolds. This is
recalled in Appendix A, in the general context of symplectic groupoids. Integral
aï¬ƒne manifolds (B, Î›) provide the simplest examples of symplectic groupoids: the
symplectic torus bundle TÎ›. These give rise to the simpler theory of Hamiltonian
TÎ›-spaces (see A.4) where one can take Corollary A.4.1 as deï¬nition. For a free
Hamiltonian TÎ›-space q : (X, â„¦X ) â†’ B with connected ï¬bers, the reduced Poisson
manifold Xred = X/TÎ› is of proper type, it will have B as (smooth) leaf space and
the induced integral structure is precisely Î› (cf. Corollary A.4.2). Looking at the
explicit s-connected integrating groupoid X â‹†

(a) it is s-proper iï¬€ q : X â†’ B is proper;
(b) it is compact iï¬€ X is compact;
(c) it is the canonical integration iï¬€ the q-ï¬bers are 1-connected.
Already when B is 1-dimensional, with the standard integral aï¬ƒne structure, pro-
duces interesting examples. For instance, the strong compact type example from
[47] arises via this procedure with B = S1. For B = R, using maps q with ï¬bers
which are not 1-connected may produce examples which are not strong proper but
are proper (or s-proper if q is proper). The previous example ï¬ts into this scheme,
but here is a slightly more general class of examples.

4. The linear variation theorem I: 1-connected leaves

The linear variation in the titles of this section and the next one refers to a
fundamental result concerning PMCTs, which is a generalization of the classical
Duistermaat-Heckman Theorem on the variation of the cohomology class of the
symplectic form of symplectic reduced spaces. Loosely speaking, we will see that
for a PMCT the cohomology class of the symplectic form on the leaves varies
linearly, relative to the integral aï¬ƒne structure on the leaf space. One can state
this result in a precise way in several distinct ways and we will see that each of
these exhibit interesting features of the PMCT and its transverse geometry.

Throughout this section we ï¬x a Poisson manifold (M, Ï€) of s-proper type and

an s-connected, s-proper integration (G, â„¦) â‡’ (M, Ï€). Moreover:

â€¢ Standing assumption in this section: The symplectic leaves of (M, Ï€)

are 1-connected.

This assumption will be dropped in the next section, where we will consider the
general case. The advantage of dealing ï¬rst with 1-connected symplectic leaves is
that there are no subtleties arising from the geometry of the leaf space: in this case
B = M/FÏ€ is smooth and there is only one groupoid integrating FÏ€, namely the
equivalence relation M Ã—B M âŠ‚ M Ã—M associated with the submersion p : M â†’ B.
Therefore we do not have to worry about the foliation groupoid B(G) (cf. Theorem
2.6.9) or, equivalently, with the orbifold structure on B, which may be present even
when B is smooth but the leaves are not 1-connected.

4.1. The developing map for integral aï¬ƒne structures. The linear variation
theorem, in one form or another, involves a basic concept of integral aï¬ƒne geometry
which we have not discussed so far. This is done in this section, where we ï¬x an
integral aï¬ƒne manifold (B, Î›) and we discuss its developing map [36, 61]. This
is a local diï¬€eomorphism of integral aï¬ƒne manifolds

dev : (eB,eÎ›) â†’ (Rq, Zq)

38

MARIUS CRAINIC, RUI LOJA FERNANDES, AND DAVID MARTÂ´INEZ TORRES

Example 4.2.6. For a Lie algebra g of compact type, the dual M = gâˆ— is a Poisson

Remark 4.3.5. The corollary shows that, for any v âˆˆ Im(dev0) âŠ‚ Rq, one can

Theorem 4.3.2 and Corollary 4.3.4 are reminiscent of a famous result of Duister-
maat and Heckman [29] stating that for Hamiltonian torus actions on symplectic
manifolds the cohomology classes of their symplectic reduced spaces vary linearly.
We will explain in detail this connection in the next section.

For now we observe that the previous results suggests the following strategy to
construct examples of PMCTs. For simplicity we restrict to integral aï¬ƒne manifolds
which are complete (see Conjecture 3.1.4).

Example 4.3.7. The simplest case to consider is B = S1 with its usual integral
aï¬ƒne structure. The resulting problem turns out to be very closely related to
McDuï¬€ and Salamonâ€™s question on the existence of symplectic free circle actions
with contractible orbits. Actually, the example given by Kotschick in that context
[42] turns out to be precisely the answer to our problem for B = S1 (see also [47]).
That produces a very interesting example of Poisson manifold of strong compact
type with leaf space S1 and K3 surfaces as symplectic leaves. As we shall explain in
future work, one can use the structure of the moduli space of marked K3 surfaces
to build similar PMCTs of strong compact type with base the standard T2 and
symplectic leaf the K3 surface (more generally, the Hilbert scheme of n points on
the K3 surface).

Remark 4.4.2 (Integral aï¬ƒne bundles). Given an aï¬ƒne bundle E â†’ B we denote
by Elin its underlying vector bundle. An integral aï¬ƒne bundle (E, Elin
Z ) is an
Z âŠ‚ Elin. As before, one can talk about
aï¬ƒne bundle E together with a lattice Elin
(weak) integral aï¬ƒne sub-bundle and of morphisms between integral aï¬ƒne bundles.
The notion of aï¬ƒne connection makes sense on any aï¬ƒne bundle E â†’ B: the
space of sections Î“(E) is an aï¬ƒne space with underlying vector space Î“(Elin) and
an aï¬ƒne connection is an aï¬ƒne map

âˆ‡ : Î“(E) â†’ â„¦1(B, Elin)

whose linear part is a linear connection on Elin. A (local) ï¬‚at section s is a section
satisfying âˆ‡s = 0 and a ï¬‚at aï¬ƒne connection is one for which there exist local
ï¬‚at sections through every point in E. If the aï¬ƒne connection is ï¬‚at, then so is it
underlying linear connection on Elin. A ï¬‚at integral aï¬ƒne bundle is an integral
aï¬ƒne bundle (E, Elin
Z ) endowed with a ï¬‚at aï¬ƒne connection âˆ‡ whose underlying
linear connection âˆ‡lin coincides with the one induced by the lattice Elin
Z .

The notion of parallel transport and its basic properties extend to the setting of
aï¬ƒne connections. In particular, a ï¬‚at integral aï¬ƒne bundle is the same thing as an
integral aï¬ƒne bundle (E, Elin
Z ) together with an action of Î 1(B) on E by integral
aï¬ƒne transformations of the ï¬bers. Also, a morphism of ï¬‚at integral aï¬ƒne
bundles is an integral aï¬ƒne morphism f : E â†’ F which is Î 1(B)-equivariant.

While any vector space/bundle is canonically aï¬ƒne, any integral vector bundle
is canonically a ï¬‚at integral aï¬ƒne bundle, with the connection associated with the
integral structure. An aï¬ƒne space/bundle is non-canonically isomorphic as aï¬ƒne
spaces/bundles to its underlying vector space/bundle, and similarly if we add the
adjective â€œintegralâ€, because an aï¬ƒne bundle always has a global section. However,
a ï¬‚at integral aï¬ƒne bundle E is not isomorphic as ï¬‚at integral aï¬ƒne vector bundles
to its underlying integral aï¬ƒne vector bundle, unless E has a global ï¬‚at section.
This is equivalent to the vanishing of the so-called radiance obstruction of the
ï¬‚at aï¬ƒne bundle [35].

We recall that a regular orbit is called principal if its isotropy is connected. A
good example to keep in mind is G = SO(3), whose non-trivial conjugacy classes
are all regular and among these there is only one which is non-principal, namely the
conjugacy class of a non-trivial diagonal matrix. Principal orbits are 1-connected,
and therefore (Gprinc, LG) is a connected regular twisted Dirac manifold of s-proper
type, satisfying our standing assumption. Hence, the leaf leaf space B = Gprinc/G
is a smooth manifold carrying an integral aï¬ƒne structure Î›B such that Î›G = pâˆ—Î›B.
Again, we can relate our previous discussion with some standard facts from the Lie
theory of conjugacy classes of compact Lie groups (see.e.g., [30, 8]).

5. The linear variation theorem II: the general case

We now extend the results from the previous section to general PMCTs of s-
proper type, removing the assumption that symplectic leaves are 1-connected. The
main diï¬€erence is that now the leaf space is an orbifold. We will see how to state
an appropriate version of the linear variation theorem, which will be a statement
that holds on an orbifold bundle made of cohomologies of the symplectic leaves.

5.1. The developing map for transverse integral aï¬ƒne foliations. Since we
do not have a smooth leaf space anymore, we are now forced to work with transverse
integral aï¬ƒne structures. Let us point out how the discussion in Section 4.1, on
the developing map of integral aï¬ƒne manifolds, can be extended to the setting of
transversally integral aï¬ƒne foliations [36, 53, 61].

5.3. Examples.

5.3.1. The classical Duistermaat-Heckman theorem. The linear variation of coho-
mology, given by Theorems 4.3.2 and 5.2.4, or their more explicit versions Corol-
laries 4.3.4 and 5.2.5, are the global versions of a famous result of Duistermaat and
Heckman [29]: if a torus T acts freely on a symplectic manifold S in a Hamiltonian
fashion then, in the integral aï¬ƒne coordinates of tâˆ—, the cohomology classes of the
reduced spaces vary linearly with slopes the Chern classes.

More precisely, we can recover the classical Duistermaat-Heckman theorem as
follows. Given a free Hamiltonian T-action on a connected symplectic manifold
(S, Ï‰) with moment map Âµ : S â†’ tâˆ— we consider the Poisson manifold M = S/T,
whose symplectic leaves are precisely the symplectic reduced spaces SÎ¾ = Âµâˆ’1(Î¾)/T
(see Appendix A.2). This kind of Poisson manifolds furnish examples of PMCTs,
and were discussed in detail in [20, Section 5.4]: there it is shown that if Âµ is proper,
then an s-connected, s-proper symplectic integration is:

G = (S Ã—Âµ S)/T â‡’ S/T,

with symplectic form â„¦ induced from prâˆ—

1 Ï‰ âˆ’ prâˆ—

2 Ï‰.

Now observe that for this symplectic integration (G, â„¦) â‡’ S/T:

â€¢ the induced orbifold structure on the leaf space B = Âµ(S) âŠ‚ tâˆ— is the

submersion groupoid B(G) = S/T Ã—Âµ S/T, so it is smooth;

â€¢ the induced integral aï¬ƒne structure on tâˆ— is the canonical integral aï¬ƒne

structure Î› for which T = tâˆ—/Î›;

â€¢ the s-ï¬ber of G through a point in SÎ¾ is (isomorphic to) the principal T-

bundle Âµâˆ’1(Î¾) â†’ SÎ¾.

Since the orbifold B(G) is actually smooth, Theorem 5.2.4 (respectively, Corollary
5.2.5), reduces to its smooth version, Theorem 4.3.2 (respectively, Corollary 4.3.4).
The conclusion is that the cohomology class [Ï‰Î¾] of the symplectic form of the

This is precisely the classical result of Duistermaat and Heckman [29].

Actually, in their paper Duistermaat and Heckman allow for non-free actions,
which leads to symplectic reduced spaces Âµâˆ’1(Î¾)/T which are orbifolds. It is not
hard to see that our work on PMCTs can be extended to Poisson orbifolds, which
allow to treat the non-free case. Moreover, our approach extends to the non-regular
case [21], showing that s-proper Poisson manifolds provide the right setting for the
globalization of the Duistermaat-Heckman theorem.

5.3.2. Regular conjugacy classes. Let us return to the Cartan-Dirac structure on

foliation FLG made of the regular conjugacy classes and its leaf space B = Greg/G
is now an integral aï¬ƒne orbifold.

6. Measures and the Duistermaat-Heckman formula

Another fundamental property of PMCTs is the existence of natural invariant
volume forms/measures. Given a regular Poisson manifold of proper type (M, Ï€)
and an s-connected, proper integration (G, â„¦) â‡’ M we will see that the leaf space
B = M/FÏ€ carries a natural measure. The basic idea is simple: an integral aï¬ƒne
structure gives rise to a density, hence to a measure. However, since B is an
orbifold we need a bit of care with the role of the groupoid in this construction.
The outcome will be that any s-connected, proper integration (G, â„¦) induces a
measure on B, called the integral aï¬ƒne measure induced by (G, â„¦) and denoted
ÂµG
Aï¬€ (we omit the dependence in â„¦ in the notation, but the reader should keep in
mind that this construction depends on having a proper symplectic integration).

In the s-proper case there is yet another natural measure on B: the one obtained
by pushing down the Liouville measure associated to the symplectic form â„¦. One
obtains a measure on B, called the Duistermaat-Heckman measure induced
by (G, â„¦) and denoted by ÂµG
Aï¬€ and
ÂµG
DH can be described via a Duistermaat-Heckman formula, involving the volumes
of the symplectic leaves.

As we shall show in [21] Theorem 6.3.1 holds for arbitrary Poisson manifolds of

Remark 6.3.5. This section is related to Weinsteinâ€™s work on measures on stacks
[64]. According to his philosophy, the measures to consider in Poisson Geometry
should arise by interpreting the symplectic groupoid as a stack. Our approach here
is more direct approach, using the foliation groupoid instead of the full symplectic
one. The precise relationship between the two is explained in [23].

7. Proper isotropic realizations

Many algebraic or geometric objects can be studied via their representations.
This philosophy also applies to Poisson Geometry, where the representations of a
Poisson manifold take the concrete form of symplectic realizations (see below). For
instance, the integrability of a Poisson manifold is equivalent to the existence of a
complete symplectic realization [18]. In this section we show that the properness of
a Poisson manifold is closely related to the existence of proper isotropic realizations.
More precisely, to any proper isotropic realization q : (X, â„¦X ) â†’ (M, Ï€) we
will associate a symplectic integration of (M, Ï€)- the holonomy symplectic groupoid
relative to X, denoted by HolX (M, Ï€). It is the smallest integration that acts on
X symplectically and it will play an import role in the last two sections of the
paper. There we will introduce the Lagrangian Dixmier-Douady class of a proper
integration and the ones with vanishing class are precisely the holonomy symplectic
groupoids relative to some proper isotropic realization.

Proper isotropic realizations appeared ï¬rst in the work of Dazord and Delzant
[26], under the name of symplectically complete isotropic ï¬brations, as special ï¬-
brations of symplectic manifolds that generalize Lagrangian ï¬brations. From that
point of view, this section generalizes the fact that the base of a proper Lagrangian
ï¬bration inherits an integral aï¬ƒne structure: we will show that the base of a proper
symplectically complete isotropic ï¬bration with connected ï¬bers is a Poisson ma-
nifold of proper type.

7.1. Symplectic realizations and Hamiltonian G-spaces. Recall that a sym-
plectic realization of a Poisson manifold (M, Ï€) is a symplectic manifold (X, â„¦X )
together with a Poisson submersion

q : (X, â„¦X) â†’ (M, Ï€).

The symplectic realization is called complete if for any complete Hamiltonian
vector ï¬eld Xh âˆˆ X(M ) the pullback Xhâ—¦q âˆˆ X(X) is complete. Of course, if q
is proper then it is complete. While every Poisson manifold admits a symplectic
realization, for complete symplectic realizations one has:

Theorem 7.1.1 ([18]). A Poisson manifold is integrable if and only if it admits a
complete symplectic realization.

Note however that, as the canonical integration Î£(M, Ï€) may already fail to be
Hausdorï¬€, in the previous theorem one has to allow for non-Hausdorï¬€ symplectic
realizations (cf. [18, Remark 1]). However, we will soon impose conditions that
ensure that all the manifolds involved are Hausdorï¬€.

We recall in Appendix A that for a symplectic integration (G, â„¦) â‡’ (M, Ï€)
the moment map of an inï¬nitesimally free Hamiltonian G-space (X, â„¦X ) yields a
symplectic realization of (M, Ï€). This motivates:

Deï¬nition 7.1.2. Given a symplectic realization q : (X, â„¦X ) â†’ (M, Ï€), a sym-
plectic integration (G, â„¦) â‡’ (M, Ï€) is called X-compatible if there is a symplectic
G-action with moment map q : X â†’ M :

7.2. The holonomy groupoid relative to an isotropic realization. In gen-
eral, for a proper Poisson manifold (M, Ï€) the canonical integration Î£(M, Ï€) â‡’
M will fail to be proper. For a symplectic realization, the holonomy groupoid
HolX (M, Ï€) constructed in the previous section is a smaller integration with better
chances of being proper. When HolX (M, Ï€) is a proper symplectic groupoid then,
according to Theorem 3.3.1, it will determine a transverse integral aï¬ƒne structure
on (M, FÏ€). We focus now on a class of proper symplectic realizations to which one
can always attach a transverse integral aï¬ƒne structure:

Deï¬nition 7.2.1. An isotropic realization of a Poisson manifold (M, Ï€) is a
symplectic realization q : (X, â„¦X ) â†’ (M, Ï€) whose ï¬bers are connected isotropic
submanifolds of (X, â„¦X).

Remark 7.2.2. Dazord and Delzant have studied in [26] the notion of a sym-
plectically complete isotropic ï¬bration of a symplectic manifold (X, â„¦X ). It
is deï¬ned as a ï¬bration q : X â†’ M satisfying two properties:

(i) the ï¬bers of q are isotropic;
(ii) the symplectic orthogonal (ker dq)âŠ¥ is an integrable distribution.

If one assumes additionally that the ï¬bers of q are connected it follows that M
carries a unique Poisson structure such that q : (X, â„¦X ) â†’ (M, Ï€) is a Poisson
map, therefore making q into an isotropic realization of (M, Ï€). Conversely, any
isotropic realization of a Poisson manifold satisï¬es Dazord-Delzantâ€™s conditions.

However, while the two notions are equivalent, they do reï¬‚ect two diï¬€erent points
of view, depending on whether one emphasizes the Poisson manifold (M, Ï€) or the
symplectic manifold (X, â„¦X ), respectively. The second point of view makes it clear
that we are dealing with a generalization of the notion of a Lagrangian ï¬bration of
a symplectic manifold (see Section 3.1).

Generalizing from proper Lagrangian ï¬brations, any proper isotropic realization
also has an associated lattice. The inï¬nitesimal action Ïƒ : qâˆ—T âˆ—M â†’ X(X) asso-

Ïƒ : Î½âˆ—(FÏ€) â†’ X(X), which integrates to a global (bundle of groups) action:

We now look at some consequences of Theorem 7.2.5. We concentrate on Poisson
manifolds (M, Ï€) for which the symplectic foliation FÏ€ is of proper type, a condition
that is necessary for (M, Ï€) to be of proper type. First of all we have:

Corollary 7.2.10. Let (M, Ï€) be a Poisson manifold with FÏ€ of proper type. If
q : (X, â„¦X ) â†’ (M, Ï€) is a proper isotropic realization then HolX (M, Ï€) is an s-
connected proper symplectic groupoid. Moreover:

(i) the orbifold structure induced on B = M/FÏ€ is a classical orbifold structure;
(ii) the transverse integral aï¬ƒne structure associated to the proper isotropic real-
ization q coincides with the one induced by the proper integration HolX (M, Ï€).

In particular:

Corollary 7.2.11. Let (M, Ï€) be a Poisson manifold with FÏ€ of proper type. If
(M, Ï€) admits a proper symplectic realization, then (M, Ï€) is of proper type.

One should be aware however that the properness of (M, Ï€) does not imply the
existence of proper symplectic realizations. As we shall explain in the ï¬nal sections
of the paper, there is one more obstruction to the existence of proper symplectic
realizations: the Lagrangian Dixmier-Douady class.

The previous corollary brings us to yet another aspect: after eventually passing
to a cover, proper symplectic realizations can be made simple in the sense of the
following:

extend to twisted Dirac structures with the appropriate modiï¬cations. Now, the
central notion is that of a presymplectic realization (see [11]):

The Dazord-Delzant theory also extends in a more or less straightforward way

to twisted Dirac structures (see [57] for the case of twisted Poisson structures).

8. Symplectic gerbes over manifolds

In the previous sections we have seen that Poisson manifolds of proper type
come with a rich transverse geometry. In particular, the leaf space is an integral
aï¬ƒne orbifold. We now ï¬x an integral aï¬ƒne orbifold (B, Î›) and we investigate
the freedom one has in building Poisson manifolds of proper type with leaf space
(B, Î›). This problem is really about constructing (symplectic) groupoid extensions
with kernel the torus bundle T associated to Î›, and hence resembles the standard
theory of S1-gerbes ([6, 45, 51, 55, 62]).

Recall that an S1-gerbe is a higher version of the notion of principal S1-bundle
over B. While principal S1-bundles are classiï¬ed by their Chern class c1 âˆˆ H 2(B, Z),
S1-gerbes are classiï¬ed by a similar class c2 âˆˆ H 3(B, Z), called the Dixmier-Douady
class. We will introduce a symplectic variant of the theory, consisting of symplectic
TÎ›-gerbes over B and we will show that they are classiï¬ed by their Lagrangian
Dixmier-Douady class living in H 2(B, T Lagr).

To achieve this, we will need the following variations of the standard theory of

S1-gerbes:

v1: Replace S1 by general torus bundles T : this is straightforward, but
note that, while S1-gerbes arise as higher versions of principal S1-bundles,
in the process of passing from S1 to torus bundles, principal S1-bundles will
be replaced by T -torsors, a special class of principal T -bundles. Therefore,
T -gerbes arise as higher versions of T -torsors;

v2: A symplectic version of the theory: this is the main novelty of our
story. Remarkably, the lower version of the theory, the symplectic story, i.e.
symplectic T -torsors, as well as its relevance to Lagrangian ï¬brations, has
already appeared in [60];

v3: Gerbes over orbifolds: although a large part of our discussion will be
carried out in the case where B is a smooth manifold, in general our leaf
spaces B are orbifolds. The passage from manifolds to orbifolds will be
based again on Haeï¬‚igerâ€™s philosophy (Remark 2.3.3). Note that S1-gerbes
over orbifolds have already been considered- see e.g. [45].

Some of the generalizations of S1-gerbes that we will consider could, in principle,
be obtained by making use of the general theory of gerbes with a given â€œbandâ€ over
general â€œsitesâ€ [7, 34, 43]. However, since all our bands will be abelian, and the
most general sites we we need are the ones associated to orbifolds, we do not have
to appeal to the general theory. And, more importantly, our symplectic gerbes can
always be represented by extensions of Lie groupoids (as in, e.g., [55, 45, 6, 62]).

In this Section we take care of symplectic gerbes over smooth manifolds, leaving
the orbifold case for the next section. Regarding PMCTs, the outcome can be
stated in a simpliï¬ed form as follows:

Theorem 8.0.5. Assume that (M, Ï€) is a Poisson manifold of proper type whose
foliation FÏ€ has 1-connected leaves. Then each proper integration (G, â„¦) â‡’ (M, Ï€)
gives rise to a symplectic gerbe over B = (M/FÏ€, Î›G), which is classiï¬ed by a class

c2(G, â„¦) âˆˆ H 2(B, T Lagr).

Moreover, its pull-back via the projection p : M â†’ B is precisely the Dazord-Delzant
class c2(M, Ï€, Î›G) (Deï¬nition 7.5.2). Furthermore, c2(G, â„¦) vanishes iï¬€ G is the
gauge groupoid of a free Hamiltonian T -space q : (X, â„¦X ) â†’ B (Appendix A.4).

8.2. Gerbes and their Dixmier-Douady class. We are now ready to move to
gerbes. Our exposition will be self-contained, overviewing the standard theory of
S1-gerbes and explaining at the same time how to take care of v1, replacing S1 by
a general torus bundle. Throughout this section we ï¬x the base manifold B and all
the objects that we will consider will be ï¬bered over B (see Remark 8.1.1).

8.2.1. Deï¬nition of a T -gerbe. Among the various approaches to gerbes, the most
relevant one for us is via extensions of groupoids [6, 45, 55, 62]: an S1-gerbe over
B is, up to Morita equivalence, an S1-extension of B, where B is interpreted as
a the identitiy groupoid. The groupoids that are Morita equivalent to B are the

8.2.4. The Dixmier-Douady class as a group isomorphism. The additivity of the
Dixmier-Douady class:

c2(G1 â‹† G2) = c2(G1) + c2(G2),

can be checked using a formal argument based on the interpretation of c2 via the
exact sequence (8.13), starting from the remark that G1 Ã—B G2 deï¬nes a T Ã—T -gerbe
over M1 Ã—B M2. Alternatively, one can also give a â€œdown to earthâ€ argument in
terms of cocycles. Again, the details of the direct approach will be given in the
next section in the context of symplectic gerbes.

Since c2(G) = 0 if and only if G represents the trivial gerbe, we conclude that c2
is an injective group homomorphism. Since the base manifold M of our extensions
is allowed to be disconnected, c2 is also surjective: any class u âˆˆ H 2(B, T ) is
represented by a Ë‡Cech cocycle {cijk} with respect to a good cover V = {Vi}iâˆˆI of
B, so taking M to be the disjoint union of the Vi, we see that {cijk} becomes a
2-cocycle on the resulting groupoid M Ã—B M with coeï¬ƒcients in TM , and we let G
to be the corresponding extension.

Putting everything together, one obtains the central result of the theory:

Theorem 8.2.6. The Dixmier-Douady class induces an isomorphism between the
group of T -gerbes over B and H 2(B, T ).

Remark 8.4.2. In general, the map (8.17) fails to be injective. Hence, it is possible
to have a proper symplectic integration (G, â„¦) â‡’ (M, Ï€) such that c2(G, â„¦) 6= 0,
but c2(M, Ï€, Î›G) = 0.
In this case, the Poisson manifold (M, Ï€) admits rather
diï¬€erent proper integrations deï¬ning the same lattice: one of them arising from
free Hamiltonian reduction (Theorem 7.5.3) and the other one not. When the
ï¬bers of p : M â†’ B (the symplectic leaves) are 1-connected, a spectral sequence
argument shows that (8.17) is injective, and we conclude that either all proper
integrations of (M, Ï€, Î›) arise from free Hamiltonian TÎ›-reduction, or none of them
does.

9. Symplectic gerbes over orbifolds

We now extend the theory of symplectic gerbes over manifolds to the case of
orbifolds. Of course, our motivation comes from the fact that the leaf spaces of
PMCTs are, in general, orbifolds. As in Section 8, we start with a simpliï¬ed
statement of our results, generalizing Theorem 8.0.5:

Theorem 9.0.3. Let (G, â„¦) â‡’ M be a proper integration of (M, Ï€), inducing the
orbifold atlas B = B(G) on the leaf space B = M/FÏ€. Then (G, â„¦) gives rise to a
symplectic gerbe over (B, B), which is classiï¬ed by a cohomology class

c2(G, â„¦) âˆˆ H 2(B, T Lagr).

Moreover, c2(G, â„¦) vanishes iï¬€ G = BX(M, Ï€), the B-integration of (M, Ï€) relative
to a proper isotropic realization q : (X, â„¦X ) â†’ (M, Ï€) (cf. Deï¬nition 7.4.1).

The passage from symplectic gerbes over manifolds to symplectic gerbes over
orbifolds is based on Haeï¬‚igerâ€™s philosophy explained in Remark 2.6.8. So through-
out this section we ï¬x an orbifold (B, B), with atlas B â‡’ N . The reader should
keep in mind, as main examples: the smooth case of the previous section where B
is B â‡’ B (hence N = B), and the leaf space of a proper Poisson manifold (M, Ï€)
with proper integration G, where B(G) â‡’ M will be the foliation groupoid from
Theorem 2.6.9 (hence N = M ).

To handle various geometric structures on the orbifold (B, B) one may, in prin-
ciple, represent them with respect to the atlas B. However, for some purposes this
may not be the most convenient atlas, so we will be looking at other orbifold atlases
E â‡’ M related to B through a speciï¬ed Morita equivalence

fold atlas. Still, Example 9.2.2 shows that if a gerbe is represented by an extension
over the atlas QE : E â‰ƒ B, with E â‡’ M , then we can pullback along any map
q : M â€² â†’ M transverse to the orbits of E. Letting q be the inclusion of a complete
transversal, we see that any gerbe can be represented over an Â´etale atlas.

Appendix A. Symplectic groupoids and moment maps

In this appendix we recall some basic notions and results associated with sym-
plectic groupoids and their actions on symplectic manifolds, which are needed
throughout the paper. Much of this material goes back to the work of Mikami
and Weinstein [49] and Xu [65], complemented by the results in [18].

A.1. Hamiltonian G-spaces. Given a Poisson manifold (M, Ï€) and an integrat-
ing symplectic groupoid (G, â„¦), a Hamiltonian G-space is a symplectic manifold
(X, â„¦X ) endowed with a smooth map

as well as an action m of G on X along q which symplectic. The condition that the
action is symplectic can be expressed by saying that its graph:

where pri are the two projections.

Hamiltonian vector ï¬eld Xh âˆˆ X(M ) the pullback Xhâ—¦q âˆˆ X(X) is complete. The
very ï¬rst basic fact about Hamiltonian G-spaces is:

Appendix B. Proper transverse integral affine foliations

In this section we show that some of the ideas from Molinoâ€™s structure theory
of riemannian foliations ([52, 53]) can be adapted to the case of transverse integral
aï¬ƒne foliations proving in particular that the leaf spaces of proper transverse inte-
gral aï¬ƒne foliations are good orbifolds, i.e. global quotients modulo proper actions
of discrete groups.
In the terminology of [61, 36], we show that proper integral
aï¬ƒne foliations are complete.

Throughout this section we ï¬x a foliated manifold (M, F ) together with a trans-

verse integral aï¬ƒne structure Î› âŠ‚ Î½âˆ—(F ).

a proper groupoid. It then suï¬ƒces to remark that if H is a proper groupoid and
F : H â†’ G is a morphism of Lie groupoids over M covering the identity on the
base, then F is automatically a proper map: for K âŠ‚ G compact, F âˆ’1(K) is closed
inside the compact H(s(K), t(K))), hence it must be compact. In particular, if F
is an injective immersion, than it is automatically a closed embedding.
(cid:3)

As explained in Example 3.2.6, under the present assumptions, B = M/F is an

integral aï¬ƒne orbifold. Our ï¬nal result shows that B is actually a good orbifold:

is a smooth integral aï¬ƒne manifold. Moreover, the action of Î“lin on M lin descends

Morita equivalence:

Proof. By Lemma B.2.2 (iv), the holonomy groupoid of (M lin, F lin) is proper and
this foliation has trivial holonomy. Hence, it must be a simple foliation with smooth
orbit space Blin. Equivalently, now the free action of Hol(M, F ) on M lin is also
proper, hence Blin = M lin/ Hol(M, F ) is smooth and q : M lin â†’ Blin is a principal
Hol(M, F )-bundle. By Lemma B.2.2 (ii) we have an action of Î“lin on Blin and
p : M lin â†’ M is a principal Blin â‹Š Î“lin-bundle.

The two actions on M lin clearly commute, hence we obtain a Morita equiva-
lence. Since properness is a Morita invariant, it follows that the action of Î“lin on
Blin must be proper. The properties concerning the integral aï¬ƒne structure are
straightforward.
(cid:3)

Remark B.3.3. There is a version of the previous theorem where the linear ho-
lonomy cover M lin is replaced by the aï¬ƒne holonomy cover M Aï¬€, giving rise to
a similar Morita equivalence of Hol(M, F ) â‡’ M with BAï¬€ â‹Š Î“Aï¬€ â‡’ BAï¬€ . Here
BAï¬€ is the aï¬ƒne holonomy cover of the integral aï¬ƒne manifold Blin. This version
allows us to view the developing map dev : M Aï¬€ â†’ Rq as the composition of the
projection M Aï¬€ â†’ BAï¬€ with the developing map dev : BAï¬€ â†’ Rq of the integral
aï¬ƒne manifold B. The argument uses the aï¬ƒne version of Lemma B.2.2.

Since a classical integral aï¬ƒne orbifold can always be obtained as the leaf space
of a foliation of proper type with a transverse integral aï¬ƒne structure, we conclude:

Corollary B.3.4. Any classical integral aï¬ƒne orbifold is a good orbifold.
